## Chapter: Chapter 4: North American Geologic History: ~250ma to ~150ma: Permian to Triassic:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we delve into the fascinating world of North American geology, specifically focusing on the period from approximately 250 million years ago to 150 million years ago, during the Permian and Triassic epochs. This era is marked by significant geological events that have shaped the continent as we know it today. 

The Permian period, spanning from 290 million years ago to 250 million years ago, was a time of intense volcanic activity and the formation of large igneous provinces (LIPs). These LIPs, such as the Selli Event and the Central Atlantic Magmatic Province (CAMP), played a crucial role in the Earth's climate and the evolution of life on land and in the oceans. 

Following the Permian, the Triassic period (250 million years ago to 200 million years ago) saw the aftermath of the Selli Event and the CAMP, with the Earth still recovering from the environmental changes these events brought about. The Triassic was also a time of significant biological recovery, with the diversification of reptiles and the rise of the dinosaurs.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore these events in detail, examining the geological processes that drove them and their lasting impacts on the North American continent. We will also discuss the methods and techniques used by geologists to study these events, providing a comprehensive understanding of the field of geology and its applications.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to the geology of the Permian and Triassic periods in North America, equipping readers with the knowledge and skills to understand and interpret the geological history of the continent. Whether you are a student, a professional geologist, or simply someone with a keen interest in geology, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your exploration of North American geology.




### Section: 3.2a Overview of the Permian Period

The Permian period, spanning from approximately 298.9 ± 0.15 to 252.17 ± 0.06 million years ago, was a time of significant geological activity. It was a period of intense plate movements, leading to the formation of new landmasses and the extinction of many species. The Permian period is divided into three epochs: the Cisuralian, Guadalupian, and Lopingian.

#### 3.2a.1 Geological Events of the Permian Period

The Permian period was marked by several significant geological events. The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, led to a spike in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was followed by the Cap Carbonate Event, which was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

The Cap Carbonate Event was followed by the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), which was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was followed by the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise, which led to the formation of carbonate platforms.

The Permian period also saw the formation of the Selli Event, which was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was followed by the Cap Carbonate Event, which was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

#### 3.2a.2 Extinctions and Evolution of Life During the Permian Period

The Permian period was also marked by several major extinction events. The Selli Event led to the extinction of many species, including the trilobites. The Cap Carbonate Event was followed by the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), which led to the extinction of many species, including the ammonoids.

The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise led to the extinction of many species, including the brachiopods. The Permian period also saw the evolution of new species, including the ammonoids and the dinosaurs.

#### 3.2a.3 Geological Formations of the Permian Period

The Selli Event led to the formation of the Selli Event deposits, which are characterized by an increase in iron availability. The Cap Carbonate Event led to the formation of the Cap Carbonate deposits, which are characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) led to the formation of the Guadalupian OAE deposits, which are characterized by an increase in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise led to the formation of the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise deposits, which are characterized by the formation of carbonate platforms.

### Section: 3.2b Major Geological Events during the Permian Period

The Permian period was a time of significant geological activity, with several major events shaping the Earth's surface and the life on it. These events include the Selli Event, the Cap Carbonate Event, the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

#### 3.2b.1 The Selli Event

The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a spike in marine productivity, which was likely driven by an increase in iron availability due to an increase in hydrogen sulphide production. The Selli Event was followed by the Cap Carbonate Event, which was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

#### 3.2b.2 The Cap Carbonate Event

The Cap Carbonate Event, which occurred during the Guadalupian epoch, was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The Cap Carbonate Event was followed by the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), which was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability.

#### 3.2b.3 The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE)

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The OAE was followed by the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise, which led to the formation of carbonate platforms.

#### 3.2b.4 The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise

The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise, which occurred during the Lopingian epoch, was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise was followed by the Cap Carbonate Event, which was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

#### 3.


### Section: 3.3 Paleogeography:

Paleogeography is the study of the Earth's surface in the past, specifically focusing on the distribution of land and water. During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

#### 3.3a Understanding Paleogeography

The Permian period was a time of intense geological activity, with several major events shaping the Earth's surface and the life on it. These events include the Selli Event, the Cap Carbonate Event, the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a spike in marine productivity, which was likely driven by an increase in hydrogen sulphide production. The Selli Event was followed by the Cap Carbonate Event, which was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

The Cap Carbonate Event, which occurred during the Guadalupian epoch, was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The Cap Carbonate Event was followed by the Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), which was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability.

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The OAE was followed by the Guadalupian Sea Level Rise, which led to the formation of carbonate platforms.

These events had a significant impact on the Earth's surface, leading to changes in sea level, ocean chemistry, and marine productivity. The Selli Event, for example, led to an increase in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability, while the Cap Carbonate Event and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise both led to changes in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms.

In addition to these events, the ongoing Antler Orogeny also played a role in shaping the Earth's surface during the Permian period. The Antler Orogeny was a period of mountain building and crustal deformation that occurred in the western United States and Canada. This event was driven by the collision of the North American and Pacific plates, leading to the formation of the Rocky Mountains and other mountain ranges.

The Antler Orogeny had a significant impact on the Earth's surface, leading to changes in landforms and the distribution of land and water. The collision of the North American and Pacific plates resulted in the uplift of the Rocky Mountains, creating a barrier between the Pacific and Arctic oceans. This led to changes in ocean currents and the formation of new landforms, such as valleys and basins.

In conclusion, the Permian period was a time of significant geological activity, with several major events shaping the Earth's surface and the life on it. The Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise all played a role in changing the Earth's surface, while the ongoing Antler Orogeny led to changes in landforms and the distribution of land and water. Understanding these events is crucial for understanding the Earth's history and the processes that shape our planet.





### Section: 3.3 Paleogeography:

Paleogeography is a crucial aspect of understanding the Earth's history and the changes that have occurred over time. During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

#### 3.3b Paleogeography of North America during the Permian Period

The Permian period was a time of intense geological activity in North America. The ongoing Antler Orogeny, which began in the late Carboniferous, continued to shape the Earth's surface during the Permian. This orogeny was characterized by the uplift of the Ancestral Rockies in Colorado, Utah, and Wyoming, as well as the formation of the Appalachian Mountains in the eastern United States.

The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America. This event was characterized by an increase in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This increase in productivity was likely driven by an increase in hydrogen sulphide production, which led to a spike in marine productivity.

The Cap Carbonate Event, which occurred during the Guadalupian epoch, was another important event in the Permian. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of carbonate platforms in the Gulf of Mexico and the western interior seaway.

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The OAE had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of carbonate platforms in the Gulf of Mexico and the western interior seaway.

The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise, which occurred at the end of the Permian, led to the formation of carbonate platforms in the Gulf of Mexico and the western interior seaway. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of carbonate platforms in the Gulf of Mexico and the western interior seaway.

Overall, the Permian period was a time of intense geological activity in North America, with several major events shaping the Earth's surface. These events had a significant impact on the Earth's surface, leading to changes in sea level, the formation of carbonate platforms, and the ongoing Antler Orogeny. Understanding these events is crucial for understanding the Earth's history and the changes that have occurred over time.

Through the study of geologic maps and field observations, we have gained a deeper understanding of the Earth's processes and how they have shaped the world we live in today.

## Chapter: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

### Introduction:

In this chapter, we will explore the geologic history of North America from 250 million years ago to the present. This time period is known as the Mesozoic era, and it is divided into three major periods: the Triassic, Jurassic, and Cretaceous. During this era, the Earth underwent significant changes in its physical and biological makeup, leading to the formation of the modern continents and the diversification of life on land and in the oceans.

We will begin by examining the Triassic period, which lasted from 250 million to 200 million years ago. This was a time of intense volcanic activity and plate movements, resulting in the formation of new landmasses and the breakup of Pangaea, the supercontinent that existed during the Permian period. We will also explore the diverse ecosystems that existed during this time, including the rise of the dinosaurs and the evolution of early mammals.

Next, we will delve into the Jurassic period, which spanned from 200 million to 145 million years ago. This was a period of relative stability in the Earth's crust, with the continents continuing to drift and the formation of new landforms. We will also examine the flourishing of life during this time, including the dominance of dinosaurs and the rise of marine reptiles.

Finally, we will conclude this chapter by exploring the Cretaceous period, which lasted from 145 million to 66 million years ago. This was a time of dramatic changes in the Earth's climate and the extinction of many species, including the non-avian dinosaurs. We will also discuss the formation of the modern continents and the rise of mammals as the dominant land animals.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a comprehensive understanding of the geologic history of North America during the Mesozoic era. You will also gain insight into the processes that shaped the Earth and the life that inhabited it during this time. So let us begin our journey through the Mesozoic and discover the wonders of this ancient world.


## Chapter 4: Mesozoic: 250ma to Present:




### Section: 3.4 Fossil Record:

The fossil record is a crucial tool for understanding the Earth's history and the changes that have occurred over time. During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

#### 3.4a Understanding the Fossil Record

The fossil record is a collection of fossils and their associated data that provide information about the Earth's past. These records are found in sedimentary rocks and can be used to reconstruct the environment and organisms that existed during a particular time period. The fossil record is a valuable tool for understanding the Earth's history, as it allows us to study the changes that have occurred over time.

During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise. These events had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity.

The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America. This event was characterized by an increase in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This increase in productivity was likely driven by an increase in hydrogen sulphide production, which led to a spike in marine productivity. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the increase in marine productivity in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Cap Carbonate Event, which occurred during the Guadalupian epoch, was another important event in the Permian. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of carbonate platforms in the Gulf of Mexico and the western interior seaway. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the formation of carbonate platforms in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) was a period of intense marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the increase in marine productivity in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise was another important event in the Permian. This event was characterized by a rise in sea level, which led to the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

In conclusion, the fossil record is a crucial tool for understanding the Earth's history and the changes that have occurred over time. During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise. The fossil record of these events can be seen in the changes in marine productivity and the formation of new landforms in sedimentary rocks from this time period. 





### Section: 3.4 Fossil Record:

The fossil record is a crucial tool for understanding the Earth's history and the changes that have occurred over time. During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise.

#### 3.4a Understanding the Fossil Record

The fossil record is a collection of fossils and their associated data that provide information about the Earth's past. These records are found in sedimentary rocks and can be used to reconstruct the environment and organisms that existed during a particular time period. The fossil record is a valuable tool for understanding the Earth's history, as it allows us to study the changes that have occurred over time.

During the Permian period, the Earth's surface underwent significant changes due to the ongoing Antler Orogeny and the effects of the Selli Event, Cap Carbonate Event, Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE), and Guadalupian Sea Level Rise. These events had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America, leading to the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity.

The Selli Event, which occurred at the end of the Cisuralian epoch, had a significant impact on the Earth's surface in North America. This event was characterized by an increase in marine productivity due to an increase in iron availability. This increase in productivity was likely driven by an increase in hydrogen sulphide production, which led to a spike in marine productivity. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the increase in marine productivity in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Cap Carbonate Event, which occurred during the Guadalupian epoch, was another important event in the Permian. This event was characterized by a drop in sea level and the formation of carbonate platforms. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's surface, leading to the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the formation of carbonate platforms in sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Guadalupian Oceanic Anoxic Event (OAE) was a major event that occurred during the Guadalupian epoch. This event was characterized by a decrease in oceanic oxygen levels, leading to anoxic conditions in the ocean. This event had a significant impact on marine life, as many organisms were unable to survive in these anoxic conditions. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the presence of anoxic sedimentary rocks from this time period.

The Guadalupian Sea Level Rise was another important event that occurred during the Permian period. This event was characterized by a rise in sea level, leading to the flooding of low-lying areas and the formation of new coastlines. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's surface, leading to the formation of new landforms and changes in marine productivity. The fossil record of this event can be seen in the presence of marine sedimentary rocks from this time period.

Overall, the fossil record provides valuable insights into the changes that occurred during the Permian period. By studying the fossil record, we can gain a better understanding of the Earth's history and the processes that shaped it. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the geologic history of North America from approximately 340 million years ago to 250 million years ago. This time period saw significant changes in the Earth's crust, including the formation of the Antler Orogeny and the rise of the Permian supercontinent. We have also discussed the various processes that shaped the Earth's surface during this time, such as plate tectonics, volcanism, and sedimentation.

The Antler Orogeny, which occurred between 340 and 320 million years ago, was a major mountain-building event that affected the entire North American continent. This orogeny was driven by the collision of the North American and Baltic plates, resulting in the formation of the Appalachian Mountains and the creation of the Hudson Bay basin. The Antler Orogeny also played a crucial role in the development of the Permian supercontinent, as it helped to unite the North American and South American plates.

The Permian supercontinent, which existed from approximately 290 to 250 million years ago, was the largest landmass in Earth's history. It was formed by the collision of the Gondwana and Laurasia supercontinents, resulting in the fusion of the continents into a single landmass. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's climate, as it led to the formation of a large land bridge between North America and Europe, allowing for the exchange of flora and fauna between the two continents.

Throughout this chapter, we have also discussed the various processes that shaped the Earth's surface during this time. Plate tectonics played a crucial role in the formation of the Antler Orogeny and the Permian supercontinent, while volcanism and sedimentation helped to shape the Earth's crust and create new landforms.

In conclusion, the geologic history of North America from approximately 340 to 250 million years ago was a time of significant change and development. The formation of the Antler Orogeny and the rise of the Permian supercontinent had a profound impact on the Earth's surface and continue to shape the landscape of North America today. By studying this time period, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet and continue to shape it today.

## Chapter: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the geologic history of North America from approximately 250 million years ago to the present day. This time period is known as the Mesozoic Era, and it is divided into three main periods: the Triassic, Jurassic, and Cretaceous. During this era, the Earth's continents underwent significant changes, including the formation of the supercontinent Pangea and the eventual breakup of this continent into smaller landmasses. This chapter will cover the major events and processes that shaped the Earth's crust during the Mesozoic Era, including plate tectonics, volcanism, and sedimentation. We will also discuss the impact of these events on the development of life on Earth, as well as the role of geologic mapping in understanding the geologic history of North America. 


# Title: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

## Chapter 4: Mesozoic Era: 250ma to Present: Pangea, Triassic, Jurassic, Cretaceous




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the geologic history of North America from approximately 340 million years ago to 250 million years ago. This time period saw significant changes in the Earth's crust, including the formation of the Antler Orogeny and the rise of the Permian supercontinent. We have also discussed the various processes that shaped the Earth's surface during this time, such as plate tectonics, volcanism, and sedimentation.

The Antler Orogeny, which occurred between 340 and 320 million years ago, was a major mountain-building event that affected the entire North American continent. This orogeny was driven by the collision of the North American and Baltic plates, resulting in the formation of the Appalachian Mountains and the creation of the Hudson Bay basin. The Antler Orogeny also played a crucial role in the development of the Permian supercontinent, as it helped to unite the North American and South American plates.

The Permian supercontinent, which existed from approximately 290 to 250 million years ago, was the largest landmass in Earth's history. It was formed by the collision of the Gondwana and Laurasia supercontinents, resulting in the fusion of the continents into a single landmass. This event had a significant impact on the Earth's climate, as it led to the formation of a large land bridge between North America and Europe, allowing for the exchange of flora and fauna between the two continents.

Throughout this chapter, we have also discussed the various processes that shaped the Earth's surface during this time. Plate tectonics played a crucial role in the formation of the Antler Orogeny and the Permian supercontinent, while volcanism and sedimentation helped to shape the Earth's crust and create new landforms.

In conclusion, the geologic history of North America from approximately 340 to 250 million years ago was a time of significant change and development. The formation of the Antler Orogeny and the rise of the Permian supercontinent had a profound impact on the Earth's surface and continue to shape the landscape of North America today. By studying this time period, we can gain a better understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet and continue to shape it today.

## Chapter: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the geologic history of North America from approximately 250 million years ago to the present day. This time period is known as the Mesozoic Era, and it is divided into three main periods: the Triassic, Jurassic, and Cretaceous. During this era, the Earth's continents underwent significant changes, including the formation of the supercontinent Pangea and the eventual breakup of this continent into smaller landmasses. This chapter will cover the major events and processes that shaped the Earth's crust during the Mesozoic Era, including plate tectonics, volcanism, and sedimentation. We will also discuss the impact of these events on the development of life on Earth, as well as the role of geologic mapping in understanding the geologic history of North America. 


# Title: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

## Chapter 4: Mesozoic Era: 250ma to Present: Pangea, Triassic, Jurassic, Cretaceous




# Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping":

## Chapter 4: North American Geologic History: ~250ma to ~present: Permian to Present:

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of North American geologic history, specifically focusing on the time period from ~250 million years ago (ma) to the present. This time frame covers the Permian to Present era, a crucial period in the evolution of our planet's geology. 

The Permian period, which lasted from ~290 to ~250 ma, was a time of significant changes in the Earth's climate and continents. It was marked by the formation of the supercontinent Pangea, the rise of the first amphibians, and the extinction of many marine species. The Permian period also saw the development of the first coal deposits, which have been a vital source of energy for millions of years.

As we move forward in time, we will explore the Triassic period (~250 to ~200 ma), which was characterized by the breakup of Pangea and the formation of the Atlantic Ocean. This period also saw the rise of the dinosaurs and the development of the first reptiles.

The Jurassic period (~200 to ~145 ma) was a time of continued geological activity, with the formation of the Alps and the Himalayas. This period also saw the rise of the first mammals and the development of the first flowering plants.

The Cretaceous period (~145 to ~65 ma) was a time of great diversity in the Earth's oceans, with the rise of the first marine reptiles and the development of the first birds. This period also saw the formation of the Rocky Mountains and the Appalachian Mountains.

Finally, we will explore the Tertiary period (~65 ma to ~2.588 ma), which saw the extinction of the dinosaurs and the rise of the first primates. This period also saw the formation of the Great Basin and the development of the first modern humans.

Throughout this chapter, we will use the popular Markdown format to present our information, with math equations rendered using the MathJax library. This will allow us to present complex geological concepts in a clear and concise manner. We will also include images and diagrams to aid in our explanations.

Join us as we journey through the fascinating world of North American geologic history, from the Permian to the Present.




### Section: 4.1a Overview of the Permian Period

The Permian period, spanning from ~290 to ~250 million years ago, was a time of significant geological and biological changes. It was a time of supercontinents, massive volcanic activity, and the rise and fall of diverse ecosystems.

#### 4.1a.1 Supercontinent Pangea

During the Permian, all the Earth's major land masses, except portions of East Asia, were collected into a single supercontinent known as Pangaea. Pangaea straddled the equator and extended toward the poles, with a corresponding effect on ocean currents in the single great ocean ("Panthalassa", the "universal sea"), and the Paleo-Tethys Ocean, a large ocean that was between Asia and Gondwana. The Cimmeria continent rifted away from Gondwana and drifted north to Laurasia, causing the Paleo-Tethys to shrink. A new ocean was growing on its southern end, the Tethys Ocean, an ocean that would dominate much of the Mesozoic Era.

#### 4.1a.2 Volcanic Activity

The Permian was also a time of massive volcanic activity. The Selli Event, a major volcanic event that occurred around 260 million years ago, led to a spike in sulphate levels in the atmosphere, which in turn caused a drop in seawater pH and carbonate saturation. This event is believed to have caused a drop in marine diversity, with many species going extinct.

#### 4.1a.3 Life in the Permian

The Permian marine deposits are rich in fossil mollusks, brachiopods, and echinoderms. Brachiopods were highly diverse during the Permian. The extinct order Productida was the predominant group of Permian brachiopods, accounting for up to about half of all Permian brachiopod genera. Amongst ammonoids, Goniatitida were a major group during the Early-Mid Permian, but declined during the Late Permian. Members of the order Prolecanitida were less diverse. The Ceratitida originated from the family Daraelitidae within Prolecanitida during the mid-Permian, and extensively diversified during the Late Permian. Only three families of trilobite are known from the Permian, Proetidae, Brachymetopidae and Phillipsiidae. Diversity, origination and extinction rates during the Early Permian were low. Trilobites underwent a diversification during the Kungurian-Wordian, the last in their evolutionary history, before declining during the Late Permian. By the Changhsingian, only a handful (4-6) genera were left.

#### 4.1a.4 The End-Permian Extinction Event

The Permian period ended with a catastrophic extinction event, known as the End-Permian Extinction Event or the Great Dying. This event, which occurred around 252 million years ago, is believed to have been caused by a combination of factors, including volcanic activity, changes in sea level, and anoxia. The End-Permian Extinction Event led to the loss of up to 90% of marine species and 70% of terrestrial vertebrate species.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the geological and biological events that occurred during the Triassic period, the next chapter in the history of North America.




### Section: 4.1b Major Geological Events during the Permian Period

The Permian period was marked by several significant geological events that shaped the Earth's surface and influenced the course of life on our planet. These events include the Selli Event, the rise of the Permian Reef Complex, and the formation of the Pangean supercontinent.

#### 4.1b.1 The Selli Event

The Selli Event, named after the Selli Formation in South Africa, was a major volcanic event that occurred around 260 million years ago. This event led to a spike in sulphate levels in the atmosphere, which in turn caused a drop in seawater pH and carbonate saturation. This drop in pH and carbonate saturation was so severe that it caused a drop in marine diversity, with many species going extinct. The Selli Event is believed to have been caused by the release of carbon dioxide from volcanic activity, which led to a greenhouse effect and a rise in global temperatures.

#### 4.1b.2 The Rise of the Permian Reef Complex

The Permian period was a time of major reef building, which transformed the Permian Reef Complex into a major reef system. This complex is composed of 95% Permian-aged rock formations and is found in the Permian Basin, a large sedimentary basin in North America. The Permian Basin is located on the western edge of the supercontinent Pangaea, within 5–10 degrees of the equator. The formation of the Permian Reef Complex was influenced by tectonic activity, as the supercontinent of Pangaea began to break up during this period.

#### 4.1b.3 The Formation of the Pangean Supercontinent

The Permian period was also a time of significant tectonic activity, as the supercontinent of Pangaea began to form. Pangaea was a single landmass that encompassed all the Earth's major land masses, except portions of East Asia. This supercontinent was surrounded by the superocean Panthalassa, with the Permian Basin located on its western edge. The formation of Pangaea had a profound impact on the Earth's climate, as it shifted the location of the continents near the equator and led to a warmer global climate.

#### 4.1b.4 The End-Permian Extinction Event

The Permian period ended with a catastrophic extinction event, known as the End-Permian Extinction Event. This event, which occurred around 251 million years ago, was the most severe extinction event in the history of life on Earth. It led to the loss of up to 90% of marine species and 70% of terrestrial vertebrate species. The cause of this extinction event is still debated, but it is believed to have been caused by a combination of factors, including volcanic activity, changes in sea level, and changes in climate.

In conclusion, the Permian period was a time of significant geological and biological changes. The events that occurred during this period, including the Selli Event, the rise of the Permian Reef Complex, the formation of the Pangean supercontinent, and the End-Permian Extinction Event, have had a profound impact on the Earth's surface and the course of life on our planet.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the geological history of North America from the Permian period to the present. We have seen how the Earth's crust has evolved over time, with the formation of new landmasses and the movement of continents. We have also examined the various geological processes that have shaped the North American landscape, including volcanic activity, erosion, and sedimentation.

We have learned about the Permian period, a time of significant geological change. During this period, the supercontinent Pangea began to break up, leading to the formation of new landmasses and the redistribution of life on Earth. We have also discussed the Triassic period, a time of intense volcanic activity and the rise of the dinosaurs.

Moving into the Mesozoic era, we have explored the Jurassic period, a time of relative stability and the rise of the angiosperms. We have also examined the Cretaceous period, a time of great diversity and the final days of the dinosaurs.

Finally, we have delved into the Cenozoic era, a time of continued geological activity and the rise of mammals. We have discussed the Oligocene, Miocene, Pliocene, and Pleistocene epochs, each with their own unique characteristics and geological events.

Through this chapter, we have gained a comprehensive understanding of the geological history of North America. We have seen how the Earth's crust has evolved over time, and how various geological processes have shaped the landscape we see today. This knowledge is crucial for any aspiring geologist, as it provides a foundation for understanding the complex processes that continue to shape our planet.

## Chapter: Chapter 5: The Geology of the United States

### Introduction

The United States, a vast and diverse country, is home to a rich tapestry of geological wonders. From the towering mountains of the Rockies to the rolling hills of the Midwest, from the sandy beaches of the East Coast to the volcanic landscapes of the West, the geology of the United States is a fascinating and complex subject. This chapter, "The Geology of the United States," aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the geological history and features of the United States.

The United States is a country of contrasts, and its geology reflects this diversity. The country is home to a variety of geological formations, including igneous rocks, sedimentary rocks, and metamorphic rocks. These rocks have been shaped by a variety of geological processes, including erosion, weathering, and volcanic activity. The United States also has a rich history of mineral resources, including gold, silver, copper, and coal, which have played a significant role in the country's economic development.

In this chapter, we will explore the geological history of the United States, from its formation as a continent to the present day. We will delve into the processes that have shaped the United States' geology, including plate tectonics, mountain building, and erosion. We will also examine the various geological features of the United States, including its mountains, valleys, and plains.

We will also discuss the impact of human activities on the geology of the United States. From mining and quarrying to urban development, human activities have had a profound effect on the geological landscape of the United States. We will explore these impacts and discuss ways in which we can mitigate them to preserve the geological heritage of the United States for future generations.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive and accessible introduction to the geology of the United States. Whether you are a student, a teacher, or simply a curious reader, we hope that this chapter will deepen your understanding and appreciation of the geological wonders of the United States.




### Section: 4.2a Overview of the Mesozoic Era

The Mesozoic Era, spanning from approximately 250 million years ago to 65 million years ago, was a time of significant geological and biological changes. This era is divided into three periods: the Triassic, Jurassic, and Cretaceous. The Mesozoic is known for its diverse and dynamic landscapes, as well as its role in the evolution of life on Earth.

#### 4.2a.1 The Triassic Period

The Triassic Period, from 250 to 200 million years ago, was the first period of the Mesozoic Era. It was a time of intense volcanic activity, with the formation of the Selli Event and the rise of the Permian Reef Complex. The Triassic was also marked by the breakup of the supercontinent Pangaea, leading to the formation of the Atlantic Ocean.

The Triassic is known for its diverse and dynamic landscapes, with deserts in the interior of Pangaea and coastal regions with lush vegetation. The period was also marked by the rise of the dinosaurs, which became the dominant land vertebrates during this time.

#### 4.2a.2 The Jurassic Period

The Jurassic Period, from 200 to 145 million years ago, was a time of continued volcanic activity and the continued breakup of Pangaea. The Jurassic is known for its diverse and dynamic landscapes, with the formation of the Alps and other mountain ranges.

The Jurassic was also a time of significant biological changes, with the evolution of new species and the spread of plants and animals across the newly formed landmasses. The period is also known for its rich fossil record, providing valuable insights into the lives of ancient creatures.

#### 4.2a.3 The Cretaceous Period

The Cretaceous Period, from 145 to 65 million years ago, was the final period of the Mesozoic Era. It was a time of continued volcanic activity and the final breakup of Pangaea, leading to the formation of the modern continents.

The Cretaceous is known for its diverse and dynamic landscapes, with the formation of the Rocky Mountains and other mountain ranges. The period was also marked by the rise of the angiosperms, or flowering plants, and the continued evolution of the dinosaurs.

The Cretaceous also saw the final extinction event of the Mesozoic Era, the Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event, which led to the extinction of the non-avian dinosaurs and many other species. This event marked the end of the Mesozoic Era and the beginning of the Cenozoic Era.

### Subsection: 4.2b Major Geological Events during the Mesozoic Era

The Mesozoic Era was marked by several significant geological events that shaped the Earth's surface and influenced the course of life on our planet. These events include the breakup of Pangaea, the formation of the Atlantic Ocean, and the rise of the Alps and other mountain ranges.

#### 4.2b.1 The Breakup of Pangaea

The breakup of Pangaea, the supercontinent that formed during the Permian Period, began during the Triassic Period and continued throughout the Mesozoic Era. This process was driven by plate tectonics, as the movement of the Earth's plates caused the continents to drift apart. The breakup of Pangaea led to the formation of the Atlantic Ocean and the rearrangement of the continents into their modern configurations.

#### 4.2b.2 The Formation of the Alps and Other Mountain Ranges

The Mesozoic Era was also a time of significant mountain building, as the Alps and other mountain ranges began to form. These mountains were formed by the collision of the African and European plates, which caused the uplift of the Earth's crust and the formation of mountain ranges. The formation of these mountains had a profound impact on the Earth's climate and the distribution of life on the planet.

#### 4.2b.3 The Cretaceous-Paleogene Extinction Event

The Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event, also known as the K-T extinction event, was the final extinction event of the Mesozoic Era. This event, believed to have been caused by an asteroid impact, led to the extinction of the non-avian dinosaurs and many other species. This event marked the end of the Mesozoic Era and the beginning of the Cenozoic Era.

### Conclusion

The Mesozoic Era was a time of significant geological and biological changes, marked by the breakup of Pangaea, the formation of the Atlantic Ocean, and the rise of the Alps and other mountain ranges. This era also saw the evolution of the dinosaurs and the final extinction event of the Mesozoic Era, the Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event. The study of the Mesozoic Era provides valuable insights into the processes that shape our planet and the evolution of life on Earth.





### Section: 4.2b Major Geological Events during the Mesozoic Era

The Mesozoic Era was a time of significant geological events that shaped the Earth as we know it today. These events include the breakup of the supercontinent Pangaea, the formation of the Atlantic Ocean, and the rise and fall of the dinosaurs. In this section, we will explore some of the major geological events that occurred during the Mesozoic Era.

#### 4.2b.1 The Breakup of Pangaea

The breakup of the supercontinent Pangaea is one of the most significant geological events of the Mesozoic Era. This process began during the Triassic Period and continued throughout the Jurassic and Cretaceous Periods. The breakup of Pangaea led to the formation of the modern continents as we know them today.

The breakup of Pangaea was driven by plate tectonics, specifically the movement of the Earth's plates. As the plates moved, they pulled apart the landmass of Pangaea, creating new ocean basins and landmasses. This process also led to the formation of the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, a mountain range that runs along the floor of the Atlantic Ocean.

#### 4.2b.2 The Formation of the Atlantic Ocean

The formation of the Atlantic Ocean is another major geological event that occurred during the Mesozoic Era. As the plates continued to move, they pulled apart the landmass of Pangaea, creating a new ocean basin between the continents of North America and Europe.

The formation of the Atlantic Ocean had a significant impact on the Earth's climate and ecosystems. The opening of the ocean allowed for the exchange of warm water from the tropics to the poles, leading to a warmer global climate. This also allowed for the spread of marine life and the formation of new ecosystems.

#### 4.2b.3 The Rise and Fall of the Dinosaurs

The Mesozoic Era is also known as the "Age of Dinosaurs" due to the dominance of these creatures during this time. Dinosaurs first appeared during the Triassic Period and quickly became the most diverse and dominant land vertebrates.

The rise of the dinosaurs was likely due to the changing climate and ecosystems brought about by the breakup of Pangaea. The warmer global climate allowed for the expansion of dinosaur habitats, and the formation of new ecosystems provided more opportunities for these creatures to thrive.

However, the dinosaurs' reign came to an end during the Cretaceous Period, when a massive asteroid impact caused a global catastrophe. This event, known as the Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event, led to the extinction of many species, including all non-avian dinosaurs.

#### 4.2b.4 The Formation of the Rocky Mountains

The formation of the Rocky Mountains is another significant geological event that occurred during the Mesozoic Era. The uplift of the Rocky Mountains began during the Jurassic Period and continued throughout the Cretaceous Period.

The formation of the Rocky Mountains was likely caused by the collision of the Pacific and North American plates. As the Pacific Plate moved westward, it collided with the North American Plate, causing the uplift of the Rocky Mountains. This event also led to the formation of the Great Basin and the Colorado Plateau.

#### 4.2b.5 The Cretaceous-Paleogene Extinction Event

The Cretaceous-Paleogene extinction event, also known as the K-Pg extinction event, was a catastrophic event that occurred at the end of the Cretaceous Period. This event is believed to have been caused by a massive asteroid impact, which created the Chicxulub Crater on the Yucatan Peninsula.

The impact of the asteroid caused a global catastrophe, leading to the extinction of many species, including all non-avian dinosaurs. This event also caused changes in the Earth's climate and ecosystems, paving the way for the rise of mammals and the modern world.

### Conclusion

The Mesozoic Era was a time of significant geological events that shaped the Earth as we know it today. From the breakup of Pangaea to the rise and fall of the dinosaurs, this era was a time of great change and diversity. The events of the Mesozoic Era continue to have a lasting impact on our planet, and studying them is crucial for understanding the history and future of our planet.





### Section: 4.3a Overview of the Cenozoic Era

The Cenozoic Era, which began approximately 66 million years ago, is the current and most recent era of the Phanerozoic Eon. It is divided into three periods: the Paleogene, the Neogene, and the Quaternary. The Cenozoic Era is marked by the continued evolution of mammals, the rise of the angiosperm flora, and the formation of the modern continents.

#### 4.3a.1 The Paleogene Period

The Paleogene Period, which lasted from approximately 66 to 23 million years ago, was a time of significant geological and biological changes. The period began with a major extinction event, known as the K-T extinction, which wiped out most of the dinosaurs and many other species. This event is believed to have been caused by a large asteroid impact, which also led to significant changes in the Earth's climate and ecosystems.

During the Paleogene, the Earth's continents continued to drift and rearrange, leading to the formation of the modern continents as we know them today. The Atlantic Ocean continued to widen, and the Indian and Pacific Oceans began to form.

The Paleogene was also a time of significant biological evolution. Mammals continued to diversify and evolve, with the rise of the placental mammals. The angiosperm flora also continued to diversify, with the spread of flowering plants across the Earth's continents.

#### 4.3a.2 The Neogene Period

The Neogene Period, which lasted from approximately 23 million years ago to 11,000 years ago, was a time of continued geological and biological changes. The Earth's continents continued to drift and rearrange, with the formation of the Isthmus of Panama and the closure of the Tethys Sea.

The Neogene was also a time of significant biological evolution. The modern orders of mammals, such as the Carnivora and the Cetacea, evolved during this period. The angiosperm flora continued to diversify, with the spread of grasses and other plants across the Earth's continents.

#### 4.3a.3 The Quaternary Period

The Quaternary Period, which began approximately 11,000 years ago, is the most recent period of the Cenozoic Era. It is marked by the most recent glacial period, known as the Quaternary glaciation, which led to significant changes in the Earth's climate and ecosystems.

During the Quaternary, the Earth's continents continued to stabilize, with the formation of the modern coastlines. The Quaternary glaciation led to the formation of many of the Earth's current landforms, such as valleys, lakes, and rivers.

The Quaternary was also a time of significant biological evolution. The modern human species, Homo sapiens, evolved during this period, and the domestication of plants and animals began.

In conclusion, the Cenozoic Era has been a time of significant geological and biological changes, leading to the formation of the modern continents and the evolution of the modern human species. The study of the Cenozoic Era is crucial for understanding the Earth's history and the processes that continue to shape our planet today.





### Section: 4.3b Major Geological Events during the Cenozoic Era

The Cenozoic Era has been marked by a series of major geological events that have shaped the Earth's continents and oceans. These events have been driven by a combination of plate tectonics, volcanic activity, and changes in sea level.

#### 4.3b.1 The Great Oxygenation Event

One of the most significant events of the Cenozoic Era was the Great Oxygenation Event (GOE), which occurred approximately 2.4 billion years ago. This event marked a dramatic increase in the concentration of oxygen in the Earth's atmosphere, from less than 1% to approximately 15%. This increase in oxygen was driven by the evolution of photosynthetic bacteria, which began to produce oxygen as a byproduct of photosynthesis.

The GOE had a profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems. It allowed for the evolution of aerobic organisms, which could now thrive in the oxygen-rich environment. This led to a rapid increase in the diversity of life on Earth, with the evolution of new species and the expansion of existing ones.

#### 4.3b.2 The Cambrian Explosion

Another major event of the Cenozoic Era was the Cambrian Explosion, which occurred approximately 541 million years ago. This event marked a rapid increase in the diversity of life on Earth, with the evolution of new species and the expansion of existing ones. This explosion of life was driven by a combination of factors, including the GOE, the rise of the angiosperm flora, and the formation of the modern continents.

The Cambrian Explosion led to the evolution of many of the major animal phyla, including arthropods, mollusks, and chordates. This event has been described as the "Cambrian revolution" due to its profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems.

#### 4.3b.3 The Cretaceous-Paleogene Extinction Event

The Cretaceous-Paleogene (K-Pg) extinction event, which occurred approximately 66 million years ago, was a major event that marked the end of the Mesozoic Era and the beginning of the Cenozoic Era. This event was caused by a large asteroid impact, which led to a global drop in temperature, changes in sea level, and widespread volcanic activity.

The K-Pg extinction event led to the loss of approximately 75% of all species, including all non-avian dinosaurs. This event had a profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems, leading to the rise of new species and the evolution of new ecosystems.

#### 4.3b.4 The Rise of the Angiosperm Flora

The Cenozoic Era has also been marked by the rise of the angiosperm flora, which began during the Paleogene Period. Angiosperms, or flowering plants, are the most diverse and abundant plant group on Earth, and their evolution has had a profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems.

The rise of the angiosperm flora was driven by a combination of factors, including the GOE, the Cambrian Explosion, and the K-Pg extinction event. This event led to the expansion of existing species and the evolution of new ones, including many of the plants that we see today.

#### 4.3b.5 The Formation of the Modern Continents

The Cenozoic Era has also been marked by the continued evolution of the Earth's continents. The continents continued to drift and rearrange, leading to the formation of the modern continents as we know them today.

The formation of the modern continents has been driven by a combination of factors, including plate tectonics, volcanic activity, and changes in sea level. This event has had a profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems, leading to the evolution of new species and the expansion of existing ones.

### Conclusion

The Cenozoic Era has been a time of significant geological and biological changes on Earth. From the Great Oxygenation Event to the Cambrian Explosion, the K-Pg extinction event, and the rise of the angiosperm flora, this era has been marked by a series of major events that have shaped the Earth's continents and oceans. These events have had a profound impact on the Earth's ecosystems, leading to the evolution of new species and the expansion of existing ones. As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's history, we gain a deeper appreciation for the complex and dynamic nature of our planet.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the geological history of North America from approximately 250 million years ago to the present. We have seen how the continent has evolved over time, from its early formation as a result of plate tectonics to its current state as a diverse and complex landmass. We have also examined the various geological events that have shaped North America, such as the formation of the Appalachian Mountains and the Great Basin, as well as the effects of climate change on the continent's geography.

Through our study of North American geology, we have gained a deeper understanding of the processes that have shaped our planet. We have learned about the interconnectedness of geological events and how they can have far-reaching effects on the environment. By studying the history of North America, we can better appreciate the complexity and beauty of our planet and the importance of preserving it for future generations.

## Chapter: Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will explore the geological history of North America from approximately 1.1 billion years ago to the present. This time period is known as the Proterozoic Eon and is divided into three major eras: the Statherian, the Cryogenian, and the Phanerozoic. Each era is further divided into smaller periods, with the Statherian being the oldest and the Phanerozoic being the most recent. This chapter will cover the key events and processes that shaped the North American continent during this time, including the formation of the Earth's early oceans, the rise of multicellular life, and the development of the modern continents. We will also discuss the various methods and techniques used by geologists to study and map the geological history of North America. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of the Proterozoic Eon and its significance in the evolution of our planet.


# Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

## Chapter 5: Proterozoic Eon: 1.1 Ga to Present:




### Section: 4.4 Present Day:

The present day is a time of great change and uncertainty in the field of geology. As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's history, we are also faced with the challenges of modern society and the impact it has on our planet. In this section, we will explore the current state of geology and the issues that are shaping the future of our field.

#### 4.4a Overview of Present Day Geology

The present day is a time of great change and uncertainty in the field of geology. As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's history, we are also faced with the challenges of modern society and the impact it has on our planet. In this section, we will explore the current state of geology and the issues that are shaping the future of our field.

One of the most pressing issues facing geologists today is the impact of human activities on the Earth's climate. The burning of fossil fuels and deforestation have led to an increase in carbon dioxide levels in the atmosphere, which is causing the Earth's temperature to rise. This rise in temperature has a cascading effect on the Earth's ecosystems, leading to changes in weather patterns, sea levels, and biodiversity. As geologists, it is our responsibility to understand and communicate the potential consequences of climate change and work towards solutions to mitigate its effects.

Another important aspect of present day geology is the continued study and understanding of the Earth's history. As we continue to uncover new information and technologies, our understanding of the Earth's past continues to evolve. This includes the ongoing study of the Great Oxygenation Event and the Cambrian Explosion, as well as the exploration of new areas and time periods.

In addition to studying the Earth's history, geologists also play a crucial role in resource exploration and extraction. As our society continues to grow and develop, the demand for resources such as oil, gas, and minerals increases. Geologists are responsible for finding and evaluating these resources, as well as understanding their impact on the environment.

#### 4.4b The Role of Geology in Society

Geology plays a crucial role in society, from providing resources to understanding and mitigating natural hazards. As our society continues to grow and develop, the demand for resources such as oil, gas, and minerals increases. Geologists are responsible for finding and evaluating these resources, as well as understanding their impact on the environment.

Geology also plays a crucial role in understanding and mitigating natural hazards such as earthquakes, volcanic eruptions, and landslides. By studying the Earth's structure and processes, geologists can identify areas at risk and develop strategies to mitigate these hazards.

#### 4.4c Future of Geology

The future of geology is uncertain, but it is clear that our field will continue to play a crucial role in understanding and addressing the challenges facing our planet. As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's history, we must also work towards solutions to mitigate the impact of human activities on our planet. This includes finding ways to reduce carbon emissions, develop sustainable resource extraction practices, and better understand and prepare for natural hazards.

In addition, advancements in technology and data analysis will continue to shape the future of geology. With the use of remote sensing and artificial intelligence, we can gather and analyze vast amounts of data to better understand the Earth's structure and processes. This will allow us to make more accurate predictions and develop more effective strategies for resource extraction and hazard mitigation.

As we move forward, it is important for geologists to continue to communicate the importance of our field to society and work towards solutions to the challenges facing our planet. By doing so, we can ensure that geology remains a vital and relevant field for generations to come.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the geologic history of North America from the Permian to the present. We have seen how the Earth's crust has evolved over time, with the formation of new landmasses and the movement of continents. We have also learned about the various geological processes that have shaped the Earth's surface, such as plate tectonics, erosion, and sedimentation.

Through our study of North American geology, we have gained a deeper understanding of the complex and dynamic nature of our planet. We have seen how the Earth's history is written in the rocks and minerals that make up its surface, and how this history can be deciphered through careful observation and analysis.

As we conclude this chapter, it is important to remember that the study of geology is an ongoing and ever-evolving field. Our understanding of the Earth's history is constantly expanding as new technologies and techniques are developed. It is up to us, as geologists, to continue exploring and uncovering the mysteries of our planet's past.

# Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping

## Chapter 5: Cenozoic to Present: Present Day:




### Section: 4.4 Present Day:

The present day is a time of great change and uncertainty in the field of geology. As we continue to explore and understand the Earth's history, we are also faced with the challenges of modern society and the impact it has on our planet. In this section, we will explore the current state of geology and the issues that are shaping the future of our field.

#### 4.4b Current Geological Research and Discoveries

In addition to studying the Earth's history, geologists are also constantly conducting research and making discoveries in the present day. This research is crucial for understanding the current state of the Earth and predicting its future.

One area of current research is the study of plate tectonics. As mentioned in the previous section, the theory of plate tectonics demonstrates that the continents of the Earth are moving across the surface at the rate of a few centimeters per year. This movement is facilitated by two factors: the energy generated within the planet and the presence of a hydrosphere. With the loss of either of these, continental drift will come to a halt.

Researchers have produced several scenarios about how this will continue in the future. These geodynamic models can be distinguished by the subduction flux, whereby the oceanic crust moves under a continent. In the introversion model, the younger, interior, Atlantic Ocean becomes preferenti

Another area of current research is the impact of human activities on the Earth's climate. As mentioned in the previous section, the burning of fossil fuels and deforestation have led to an increase in carbon dioxide levels in the atmosphere, which is causing the Earth's temperature to rise. This rise in temperature has a cascading effect on the Earth's ecosystems, leading to changes in weather patterns, sea levels, and biodiversity.

Geologists are also constantly studying and monitoring volcanic activity around the world. Volcanic eruptions can have devastating effects on surrounding communities and the environment. By studying volcanic activity, geologists can better understand the potential for future eruptions and potentially predict and mitigate their impact.

In addition to these areas of research, geologists are also constantly exploring and mapping new areas of the Earth. This includes studying the geology of the deep ocean, which covers over 70% of the Earth's surface. With advancements in technology, geologists are now able to explore and map these areas, providing valuable insights into the Earth's structure and composition.

Overall, current geological research and discoveries are crucial for our understanding of the Earth and its future. By studying the Earth's history, current state, and potential future, geologists are able to make informed decisions and take action to protect our planet.

By understanding the geologic history of North America, we can gain a deeper appreciation for the complex and dynamic nature of our planet.

This orogeny, which occurred between 120 and 80 million years ago, played a significant role in shaping the geological features of the Rocky Mountains. We will delve into the history of the Rocky Mountains, from their formation as a result of the Laramide Orogeny to their current state as one of the most iconic mountain ranges in the world.

The Rocky Mountains are a major mountain range in North America, stretching from British Columbia in Canada to New Mexico in the United States. They are home to some of the most diverse and stunning landscapes in the world, including towering peaks, deep valleys, and lush forests. The Rocky Mountains are also a hotspot for geological research, with scientists continuously studying the region to better understand its complex history.

In this chapter, we will explore the various geological processes that shaped the Rocky Mountains, including plate tectonics, erosion, and sedimentation. We will also discuss the different types of rocks and minerals found in the region, as well as the various methods used for geological mapping. ## Chapter 5: Introduction to Detachment Faults and Southwestern North America:

### Introduction

Welcome to Chapter 5 of "Field Geology I: A Comprehensive Guide to Field Camp and Geologic Mapping". In this chapter, we will be exploring the fascinating world of detachment faults and their significance in the geology of Southwestern North America.

Detachment faults are a type of fault that occurs in the Earth's crust, where the rock layers above and below the fault plane are separated by a zone of intense fracturing and shearing. These faults are commonly found in areas of mountain building and can have a significant impact on the geological processes occurring in a region.

In this chapter, we will delve into the basics of detachment faults, including their formation, characteristics, and the role they play in shaping the Earth's surface. We will also explore the geological history of Southwestern North America, specifically focusing on the effects of detachment faults on the region's geology.

Through this chapter, we hope to provide a comprehensive understanding of detachment faults and their importance in the geology of Southwestern North America. So let's begin our journey into the world of detachment faults and discover the wonders they hold.




### Section: 5.1 Detachment Faults:

Detachment faults are a type of fault that occurs in the Earth's crust, where the rock layers above and below the fault plane are separated by a zone of intense fracturing and shearing. These faults are commonly found in areas of mountain building and can have a significant impact on the geological processes occurring in a region.

#### 5.1a Understanding Detachment Faults

Detachment faults are characterized by their ability to accommodate large amounts of horizontal displacement without significant vertical displacement. This is due to the presence of a zone of intense fracturing and shearing, known as the detachment zone, which allows for the separation of rock layers. This zone is typically found at the base of the overlying rock layer, and can extend to depths of several kilometers.

The formation of detachment faults is closely linked to the process of mountain building. As the Earth's crust is subjected to compressional forces, the rocks above the detachment zone are forced to move horizontally, while the rocks below remain relatively stable. This results in the formation of a detachment fault, with the overlying rock layer being pushed up and over the underlying layer.

Detachment faults play a crucial role in shaping the Earth's surface. They are responsible for the formation of many of the world's largest mountain ranges, including the Himalayas, the Andes, and the Alps. In addition, they also play a significant role in the formation of fault scarps, which are steep cliffs that mark the edge of a fault zone.

### Subsection: 5.1b Detachment Faults in Southwestern North America

The Southwestern United States is home to some of the most well-known and studied detachment faults in the world. These faults are responsible for the formation of many of the region's iconic landforms, including the Grand Canyon, the Sierra Nevada Mountains, and the Mojave Desert.

One of the most well-known detachment faults in the Southwestern United States is the San Andreas Fault. This fault is responsible for the formation of the Sierra Nevada Mountains, as well as the creation of the San Andreas Fault Zone, which extends from the Pacific Ocean to the Gulf of Mexico. The San Andreas Fault is also known for its role in the formation of the Great Valley Sequence, a series of sedimentary rocks that fill the Great Valley of California.

Another important detachment fault in the Southwestern United States is the Basin and Range Fault. This fault is responsible for the formation of the Basin and Range Province, a region of extensive faulting and uplift that stretches from southern California to central Nevada. The Basin and Range Fault is also known for its role in the formation of the Great Basin, a large endorheic basin that covers much of western Utah and Nevada.

### Subsection: 5.1c Case Studies of Detachment Faults

To further understand the role of detachment faults in shaping the Earth's surface, let's take a closer look at two case studies: the San Andreas Fault and the Basin and Range Fault.

The San Andreas Fault is a right-lateral strike-slip fault that extends for over 1,000 kilometers along the western coast of the United States. It is responsible for the formation of the Sierra Nevada Mountains, as well as the creation of the San Andreas Fault Zone. The fault is also known for its role in the formation of the Great Valley Sequence, a series of sedimentary rocks that fill the Great Valley of California.

The Basin and Range Fault is a left-lateral strike-slip fault that extends for over 1,000 kilometers across the western United States. It is responsible for the formation of the Basin and Range Province, a region of extensive faulting and uplift that stretches from southern California to central Nevada. The fault is also known for its role in the formation of the Great Basin, a large endorheic basin that covers much of western Utah and Nevada.

Through these case studies, we can see the significant impact that detachment faults have on the Earth's surface. They are responsible for the formation of many of the world's largest mountain ranges and play a crucial role in shaping the geological processes occurring in a region. By studying these faults, we can gain a deeper understanding of the complex processes that shape our planet.

### Duplexes

Thrust faults often associate three types of structures, imbricate fan structures, ramp-flat structures, and duplex structures, all of which are seen within the Lewis thrust and the Rocky Mountain thrust and fold belt. Duplex structures are common and have been located in numerous locations along the Lewis thrust. These structures are distinct due to their structurally overlapping, lenticular stacked thrust fault slices. A prime example is seen in an area that extends from the Kootenay Pass north of the border to Marias Pass found in Montana. This section shows the Lewis Thrust following a series of bedding-parallel detachment horizons with a fairly thin stratigraphic interval near the base of the Purcell Supergroup, which is also the base of the mid-Proterozoic belt. Two windows in this section showing exposures of Upper Cretaceous strata exposed beneath the Lewis Thrust occur adjacent to the Flathead Fault. Within these windows, the Lewis thrust is folded along with overlying and underlying strata in a series of northwest-trending anticlinal culminations that extend the length of the west side of the salient. Furthermore, two distinct structural levels can be seen in this section, an upper level comprising the majority of the mass of the Lewis thrust sheet which is characterized by broad open folds in relatively undeformed rocks, and a fairly thin lower level consisting of stacked imbricate, southwest-facing, sigmoidal thrust fault slices, bounded below by the Lewis thrust, and above by a separate bedding-parallel thrust called the Tombstone thrust. These culminations progressively stack up and accommodate significant lateral crustal shortening associated with the compression along the Lewis thrust fault. Another extremely similar section of this duplexing is seen at another outcrop in the Waterton Lakes area in Southwest Alberta. ## Chapter: Field Geology: A Comprehensive Guide to Geological Processes and Structures
